              </ul>
             </li>
            </ul>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr class="plainlist">

        <th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em; padding-right:0.5em;">

          <span class="wrap">
           <a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">
            Writing system
           </a>
          </span>
         </div>
        </th>
        <td style="line-height:1.35em;">
         mainly
         <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">
          Latin alphabet
         </a>
         ; also
         <br/>
         the original
         <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">
          Hebrew
         </a>
         (normally using
         <a href="/wiki/Rashi_script" title="Rashi script">
          Rashi
         </a>
         or
         <a href="/wiki/Solitreo" title="Solitreo">
          Solitreo
         </a>
         ) and
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyrillic_alphabet" title="Cyrillic alphabet">
          Cyrillic
         </a>
         ; rarely
         <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">
          Greek
         </a>
         &amp;
         <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">
          Arabic
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>

       <tr>
        <td class="noprint selfref" colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:#ddddff;padding:0.3em 0.5em;text-align:left;line-height:1.3;">
         <b>
          This article contains
          <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
           IPA
          </a>
          phonetic symbols.
         </b>
         Without proper
         <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet#Fonts" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
          rendering support
         </a>
         , you may see
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Replacement_character" title="Replacement character">
          question marks, boxes, or other symbols
         </a>
         instead of
         <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">
          Unicode
         </a>
         characters. For an introductory guide on IPA symbols, see
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">
          Help:IPA
         </a>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        Judaeo-Spanish
       </b>
       or
       <b>
        Judeo-Spanish
       </b>
       (
       <i>
        <span lang="lad-Latn" xml:lang="lad-Latn">
         <i>
          judeo-español
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">
        Hebrew script
       </a>
       :
       <span dir="rtl" lang="lad-Hebr" xml:lang="lad-Hebr">
        גֿודֿיאו-איספאנייול
       </span>
       ‎,
       <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script" title="Cyrillic script">
        Cyrillic
       </a>
       :
       <span lang="lad-Cyrl" xml:lang="lad-Cyrl">
        Ђудео-Еспањол
       </span>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lekute_Tefilot_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lekute_Tefilot-8">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ), commonly referred to as
       <b>
        Ladino
       </b>
       , is a
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance language
       </a>
       derived from
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Spanish_language" title="Old Spanish language">
        Old Spanish
       </a>
       . Originally spoken in the former territories of the
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
        Ottoman Empire
       </a>
       (the
       <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">
        Balkans
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">
        Turkey
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">
        Middle East
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">
        North Africa
       </a>
       ) as well as in
       <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">
        France
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
        Italy
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_the_Netherlands" title="Kingdom of the Netherlands">
        the Netherlands
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">
        Morocco
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
        United Kingdom
       </a>
       , today it is spoken mainly by
       <a href="/wiki/Sephardi_Jews" title="Sephardi Jews">
        Sephardic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Minority_group" title="Minority group">
        minorities
       </a>
       in more than 30 countries, with most of the speakers residing in
       <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">
        Israel
       </a>
       . Although it has no official status in any country, it has been acknowledged as a
       <a href="/wiki/Minority_language" title="Minority language">
        minority language
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">
        Bosnia and Herzegovina
       </a>
       , Israel, France and Turkey. It is also formally recognised by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_Academia_Espa%C3%B1ola" title="Real Academia Española">
        Royal Spanish Academy
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rae_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rae-9">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The core
       <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">
        vocabulary
       </a>
       of Judaeo-Spanish is
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Spanish_language" title="Old Spanish language">
        Old Spanish
       </a>
       (as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Portuguese_language" title="Old Portuguese language">
        Old Portuguese
       </a>
       is of
       <a href="/wiki/Judaeo-Portuguese" title="Judaeo-Portuguese">
        Judaeo-Portuguese
       </a>
       ) and it has numerous elements from all the old
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance languages
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">
        Iberian Peninsula
       </a>
       :
       <a href="/wiki/Navarro-Aragonese" title="Navarro-Aragonese">
        Old Aragonese
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Astur-Leonese_languages" title="Astur-Leonese languages">
        Astur-Leonese
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Catalan_language" title="Old Catalan language">
        Old Catalan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Galician-Portuguese" title="Galician-Portuguese">
        Galician-Portuguese
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mozarabic_language" title="Mozarabic language">
        Mozarabic
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The language has been further enriched by
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_language" title="Ottoman Turkish language">
        Ottoman Turkish
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">
        Semitic
       </a>
       vocabulary, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">
        Hebrew
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" title="Aramaic language">
        Aramaic
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">
        Arabic
       </a>
       , especially in the domains of
       <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">
        religion
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">
        law
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">
        spirituality
       </a>
       and most of the vocabulary for
       <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">
        new and modern
       </a>
       concepts has been adopted through
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       . Furthermore, the language is influenced to a lesser degree by other local languages of the Balkans, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">
        Bulgarian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Serbo-Croatian" title="Serbo-Croatian">
        Serbo-Croatian
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Historically, the
       <a href="/wiki/Rashi_script" title="Rashi script">
        Rashi script
       </a>
       and its cursive form
       <a href="/wiki/Solitreo" title="Solitreo">
        Solitreo
       </a>
       have been the main orthographies for writing Judaeo-Spanish. However today, it is mainly written with the Latin alphabet, though some other
       <a href="/wiki/Alphabet" title="Alphabet">
        alphabets
       </a>
       such as Hebrew and Cyrillic are still in use. Judaeo-Spanish is known by many different names, mostly:
       <i>
        Español/Espanyol
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Judió/Djudyo
       </i>
       (or
       <i>
        Jidió/Djidyo
       </i>
       ),
       <i>
        Judesmo/Djudezmo
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Sefaradhí/Sefaradi
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Ḥaketilla/Haketia
       </i>
       . In Turkey and formerly in the Ottoman Empire, it has been traditionally called
       <i>
        Yahudice
       </i>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">
        Turkish
       </a>
       , meaning the Jewish language. In Israel, Hebrew speakers usually call the language
       <i>
        (E)spanyolit
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        Ladino
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Judaeo-Spanish, once the
       <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
        trade language
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">
        Adriatic Sea
       </a>
       , the Balkans and the Middle-East and renowned for its rich literature especially in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salonika" title="Salonika">
        Salonika
       </a>
       , today is under serious threat of
       <a href="/wiki/Language_death" title="Language death">
        extinction
       </a>
       . Most
       <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">
        native speakers
       </a>
       are elderly, and the language is not transmitted to their children or grandchildren for various reasons. In some
       <a href="/wiki/Expatriate" title="Expatriate">
        expatriate
       </a>
       communities in
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">
        Latin America
       </a>
       and elsewhere, there is a threat of
       <a href="/wiki/Dialect_levelling" title="Dialect levelling">
        dialect levelling
       </a>
       resulting in extinction by assimilation into modern Spanish. It is experiencing, however, a minor revival among Sephardic communities, especially in
       <a href="/wiki/Sephardic_music" title="Sephardic music">
        music
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11">
           <a href="#Phonological_differences_from_Spanish">
            <span class="tocnumber">
             4.3
            </span>
            <span class="toctext">
             Phonological differences from Spanish
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12">
         <a href="#Morphology">

        </li>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Name">
        Name
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       In recent decades in Israel, America and Spain, the language has come to be referred to as
       <i>
        Ladino
       </i>
       (
       <span class="Unicode">
        לאדינו
       </span>
       ), literally meaning "Latin". However, some of its speakers consider that term to be incorrect.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thessaloniki_Museum_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Thessaloniki_Museum-11">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The language is also called
       <i>
        judeo-espagnol
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [note 1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        judeo-español
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DRAE_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-DRAE-13">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Sefardí
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Judío
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        Espanyol
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        Español sefardita
       </i>
       ;
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Haketia" title="Haketia">
         Haquetía
        </a>
       </i>
       (from the Arabic
       <i>
        ħaka
       </i>
       حكى, "tell") refers to the dialect of North Africa, especially
       <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">
        Morocco
       </a>
       . The dialect of the
       <a href="/wiki/Oran_(department)" title="Oran (department)">
        Oran area of Algeria
       </a>
       was called
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetuani" title="Tetuani">
         Tetuani
        </a>
       </i>
       , after the Moroccan city of
       <a href="/wiki/T%C3%A9touan" title="Tétouan">
        Tétouan
       </a>
       since many Orani Jews came from there. In Hebrew, the language is called
       <i>
        Spanyolit
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       An entry in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">
         Ethnologue
        </a>
       </i>
       claims, "The name 'Judesmo' is used by Jewish linguists and Turkish Jews and American Jews; 'Judeo-Spanish' by Romance philologists; 'Ladino' by laymen, especially in Israel; 'Haketia' by Moroccan Jews; 'Spanyol' by some others."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       That does not reflect the historical usage. In the Judeo-Spanish press of the 19th and 20th centuries the native authors referred to the language exclusively as
       <i>
        Espanyol
       </i>
       , which was also the name that its native speakers spontaneously gave to it for as long as it was their primary spoken language. More rarely, the bookish
       <i>
        Judeo-Espanyol
       </i>
       has also been used since the late 19th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The term
       <i>
        Judezmo
       </i>
       is unknown and offensive to most native speakers, and it has never been used in print in the native press. However, in limited parts of Macedonia, its former use in the past as a low-register designation in informal speech by unschooled people has been documented.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The derivation of the name
       <i>
        Ladino
       </i>
       is complicated. Before the
       <a href="/wiki/Expulsion_of_Jews_from_Spain" title="Expulsion of Jews from Spain">
        Expulsion of Jews from Spain
       </a>
       , the word meant literary Spanish, as opposed to other dialects
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       or Romance in general, as distinct from Arabic.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (The first European language grammar and dictionary, of Spanish, referred to it as
       <i>
        ladino
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        ladina
       </i>
       . In the
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">
        Middle Ages
       </a>
       , the word
       <i>
        Latin
       </i>
       was frequently used to mean simply "language", particularly one understood: a
       <i>
        latiner
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        latimer
       </i>
       meant a translator.) Following the Expulsion, Jews spoke of "the Ladino" to mean the traditional oral translation of the Bible into Old Spanish. By extension, it came to mean that style of Spanish generally in the same way that (among Kurdish Jews)
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Targum_(Aramaic_dialect)" title="Targum (Aramaic dialect)">
         Targum
        </a>
       </i>
       has come to mean
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judeo-Aramaic_language" title="Judeo-Aramaic language">
        Judeo-Aramaic
       </a>
       and (among Jews of Arabic-speaking background)
       <i>
        sharħ
       </i>
       has come to mean
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judeo-Arabic" title="Judeo-Arabic">
        Judeo-Arabic
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Informally, especially in modern Israel, many speakers use
       <i>
        Ladino
       </i>
       to mean Judaeo-Spanish as a whole. The language used to be regulated by a body called the
       <a href="/wiki/Autoridad_Nasionala_del_Ladino" title="Autoridad Nasionala del Ladino">
        Autoridad Nasionala del Ladino
       </a>
       in Israel. More strictly, however, the term is confined to the style used in translation. According to the website of the Jewish Museum of Thessaloniki,
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        Ladino is not spoken, rather, it is the product of a word-for-word translation of Hebrew or Aramaic biblical or liturgical texts made by rabbis in the Jewish schools of Spain. In these translations, a specific Hebrew or Aramaic word always corresponded to the same Spanish word, as long as no exegetical considerations prevented this. In short, Ladino is only Hebrew clothed in Spanish, or Spanish with Hebrew syntax. The famous Ladino translation of the Bible, the
        <a href="/wiki/Ferrara_Bible" title="Ferrara Bible">
         Biblia de Ferrara
        </a>
        (1553), provided inspiration for the translation of numerous Spanish Christian Bibles."
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thessaloniki_Museum_11-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Thessaloniki_Museum-11">
          [10]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       That Judaeo-Spanish
       <i>
        ladino
       </i>
       should not be confused with the
       <i>
        ladino or
        <a href="/wiki/Ladin_language" title="Ladin language">
         Ladin language
        </a>
       </i>
       spoken in part of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northeastern_Italy" title="Northeastern Italy">
        Northeastern Italy
       </a>
       , which is closely related to the
       <a href="/wiki/Romansh_language" title="Romansh language">
        Romansh language
       </a>
       of Swiss
       <a href="/wiki/Grisons" title="Grisons">
        Grisons
       </a>
       (it is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Questione_ladina" title="Questione ladina">
        disputed
       </a>
       whether or not they form a common
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhaeto-Romance_language" title="Rhaeto-Romance language">
        Rhaeto-Romance language
       </a>
       ) and has nothing to do with either Jews or Spanish beyond being a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance_language" title="Romance language">
        Romance language
       </a>
       , a property that they share with French, Italian, Portuguese and Romanian.
      </p>
      <p>
       In modern Spanish, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_Academia_Espa%C3%B1ola" title="Real Academia Española">
        Royal Spanish Academy
       </a>
       gives "Ladino" nine meanings, including five as an adjective and four as a noun, but two are obsolete:
      </p>
      <p>
       1. Adj. Astute, sagacious, cunning
       <br/>
       2. Adj. Pertaining or relating to the Ladin language.
       <br/>
       3. Adj. In El Salvador, Guatemala, Honduras, Nicaragua, and Panama, describing a
       <a href="/wiki/Mestizo" title="Mestizo">
        mestizo
       </a>
       person who speaks only Spanish.
       <br/>
       4. Adj. In El Salvador, Guatemala, Honduras, Nicaragua, and Panama, a Mestizo person.
       <br/>
       5. Adj. Obsolete: A person with facility in languages other than his/her own.
       <br/>
       6. Noun. The Ladin language spoken in South Tyrol.
       <br/>
       7. Noun. The religious language of Sephardic Jews.
       <br/>
       8. Noun. Judeo-Spanish.
       <br/>
       9. Noun. Obsolete: The archaic literary form of Spanish called "romance" or "romantic Spanish".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <br/>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">
        Origins
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       At the time of the expulsion from Spain, the day-to-day language of the Jews of different regions of the peninsula was hardly, if at all, different from that of their Christian neighbours, but there may have been some dialect mixing to form a sort of Jewish lingua franca. There was however, a special style of Spanish used for purposes of study or translation, featuring a more archaic dialect, a large number of Hebrew and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">
        Aramaic
       </a>
       loanwords and a tendency to render Hebrew word order literally (
       <i>
        ha-laylah ha-zeh
       </i>
       , meaning "this night", was rendered
       <i>
        la noche la esta
       </i>
       instead of the normal Spanish
       <i>
        esta noche
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ). As mentioned above, some authorities would confine the term "Ladino" to that style.
      </p>
      <p>
       Following the Expulsion, the process of dialect mixing continued, but Castilian Spanish remained by far the largest contributor. The daily language was increasingly influenced both by the language of study and by the local non-Jewish vernaculars, such as Greek and Turkish. It came to be known as
       <i>
        Judesmo
       </i>
       and, in that respect, the development is parallel to that of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yiddish_language" title="Yiddish language">
        Yiddish
       </a>
       . However, many speakers, especially among the community leaders, also had command of a more formal style,
       <i>
        castellano
       </i>
       , which was nearer to the Spanish at the time of the Expulsion.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Source_languages">
        Source languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Source languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish">
        Spanish
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Spanish">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The grammar, the phonology and about 60% of the vocabulary of Judaeo-Spanish are basically Spanish but, in some respects, it resembles the dialects in southern Spain and South America, rather than the dialects of Central Spain. For example, it has
       <a href="/wiki/Ye%C3%ADsmo" title="Yeísmo">
        yeísmo
       </a>
       ("she" is
       <i>
        <span lang="lad" xml:lang="lad">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/eya#Ladino" title="wikt:eya">
          eya
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        <span lang="lad" xml:lang="lad">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ella#Ladino" title="wikt:ella">
          ella
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈeja]
       </span>
       (Judaeo-Spanish), instead of
       <i>
        <span lang="es" xml:lang="es">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ella#Spanish" title="wikt:ella">
          ella
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ) as well as and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seseo" title="Seseo">
        seseo
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In many respects, it reproduces the Spanish of the time of the Expulsion, rather the modern variety, as it retains some archaic features such as the following:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Modern Spanish
        <i>
         j
        </i>
        , pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [x]
        </span>
        , corresponds to two different phonemes in Old Spanish:
        <i>
         x
        </i>
        , pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ʃ/
        </span>
        , and
        <i>
         j
        </i>
        , pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ʒ/
        </span>
        . Judaeo-Spanish retains the original sounds. Similarly,
        <i>
         g
        </i>
        before
        <i>
         e
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         i
        </i>
        remains
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ʒ/
        </span>
        , not
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [x]
        </span>
        .
        <ul>
         <li>
          Contrast
          <i>
           baṣo
          </i>
          /
          <i>
           baxo
          </i>
          ("low" or "down", with
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /ʃ/
          </span>
          , modern Spanish
          <i>
           bajo
          </i>
          ) and
          <i>
           mujer
          </i>
          ("woman" or "wife", spelled the same, with
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /ʒ/
          </span>
          ).
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        Modern Spanish
        <i>
         z
        </i>
        (
        <i>
         c
        </i>
        before
        <i>
         e
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         i
        </i>
        ), pronounced [s] or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [θ]
        </span>
        , like the "th" in English "think", corresponds to two different phonemes in Old Spanish:
        <i>
         ç
        </i>
        (
        <i>
         c
        </i>
        before
        <i>
         e
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         i
        </i>
        ), pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ts]
        </span>
        ; and
        <i>
         z
        </i>
        (in all positions), pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [dz]
        </span>
        . In Judaeo-Spanish, they are pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [s]
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [z]
        </span>
        , respectively.
        <ul>
         <li>
          Contrast
          <i>
           korasón
          </i>
          /
          <i>
           coraçón
          </i>
          ("heart", with
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /s/
          </span>
          , modern Spanish
          <i>
           corazón
          </i>
          ) and
          <i>
           dezir
          </i>
          ("to say", with
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /z/
          </span>
          , modern Spanish
          <i>
           decir
          </i>
          ).
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        In modern Spanish, the use of the letters
        <i>
         b
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         v
        </i>
        is determined partly on the basis of earlier forms of the language and partly on the basis of Latin etymology: both letters represent one phoneme (
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /b/
        </span>
        ), realised as
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [b]
        </span>
        or as
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [β]
        </span>
        , according to its position. In Judaeo-Spanish,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /b/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /v/
        </span>
        are different phonemes:
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /bɔs/
        </span>
        <i>
         voice
        </i>
        vs.
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /vɔs/
        </span>
        <i>
         you
        </i>
        .
        <i>
         v
        </i>
        is a
        <a href="/wiki/Labiodental_consonant" title="Labiodental consonant">
         labiodental
        </a>
        "v", like in English, rather than a bilabial.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Portuguese_and_other_Iberian_languages">
        Portuguese and other Iberian languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Portuguese and other Iberian languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       However, the phonology of both the consonants and part of the lexicon is, in some respects, closer to Galician-Portuguese or Catalan than to modern Spanish. That is explained by direct influence but also because all three languages retained some of the characteristics of mediaeval Ibero-Romance languages that Spanish later lost.
      </p>
      <p>
       Contrast Judaeo-Spanish
       <i>
        <span lang="lad" xml:lang="lad">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/da%C3%ADnda#Ladino" title="wikt:daínda">
          daínda
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ("still") with Portuguese
       <i>
        <span lang="pt" xml:lang="pt">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ainda#Portuguese" title="wikt:ainda">
          ainda
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       (Galician
       <i>
        <span lang="gl" xml:lang="gl">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/a%C3%ADnda#Galician" title="wikt:aínda">
          aínda
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       , Asturian
       <i>
        <span lang="ast" xml:lang="ast">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/a%C3%ADna#Asturian" title="wikt:aína">
          aína
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        <span lang="ast" xml:lang="ast">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ena%C3%ADna#Asturian" title="wikt:enaína">
          enaína
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ) and Spanish
       <i>
        <span lang="es" xml:lang="es">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/a%C3%BAn#Spanish" title="wikt:aún">
          aún
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       or the initial consonants in Judaeo-Spanish
       <i>
        <span lang="lad" xml:lang="lad">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fija#Ladino" title="wikt:fija">
          fija
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="lad" xml:lang="lad">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/favla#Ladino" title="wikt:favla">
          favla
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ("daughter", "speech"), Portuguese
       <i>
        <span lang="pt" xml:lang="pt">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/filha#Portuguese" title="wikt:filha">
          filha
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="pt" xml:lang="pt">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fala#Portuguese" title="wikt:fala">
          fala
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       (Galician
       <i>
        <span lang="gl" xml:lang="gl">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/filla#Galician" title="wikt:filla">
          filla
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="gl" xml:lang="gl">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fala#Galician" title="wikt:fala">
          fala
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       , Asturian
       <i>
        <span lang="ast" xml:lang="ast">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/f%C3%ADa#Asturian" title="wikt:fía">
          fía
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="ast" xml:lang="ast">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fala#Asturian" title="wikt:fala">
          fala
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       , Aragonese
       <i>
        <span lang="an" xml:lang="an">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/filla#Aragonese" title="wikt:filla">
          filla
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="an" xml:lang="an">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fabla#Aragonese" title="wikt:fabla">
          fabla
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       , Catalan
       <i>
        <span lang="ca" xml:lang="ca">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/filla#Catalan" title="wikt:filla">
          filla
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ), Spanish
       <i>
        <span lang="es" xml:lang="es">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/hija#Spanish" title="wikt:hija">
          hija
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="es" xml:lang="es">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/habla#Spanish" title="wikt:habla">
          habla
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       . It sometimes varied with dialect, as in Judaeo-Spanish popular songs, both
       <i>
        <span lang="lad" xml:lang="lad">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fijo#Ladino" title="wikt:fijo">
          fijo
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <span lang="lad" xml:lang="lad">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/hijo#Ladino" title="wikt:hijo">
          hijo
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ("son") are found.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Judaeo-Spanish pronunciation of
       <i>
        s
       </i>
       as "
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʃ]
       </span>
       " before a "k" sound or at the end of certain words (such as
       <i>
        <span lang="lad" xml:lang="lad">
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/seis#Ladino" title="wikt:seis">
          seis
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       , pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [seʃ]
       </span>
       , for six) is shared with Portuguese (as spoken in Portugal, most of Asia and Africa, and in a plurality of Brazilian registers with either partial or total forms of coda |S| palatalization) but not with Spanish.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hebrew_and_Aramaic">
        Hebrew and Aramaic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Hebrew and Aramaic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Like other Jewish vernaculars, Judaeo-Spanish incorporates many Hebrew and Aramaic words, mostly for religious concepts and institutions. Examples are
       <i>
        Haham
       </i>
       (rabbi, from Hebrew
       <i>
        ḥakham
       </i>
       ) and
       <i>
        kal
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        cal
       </i>
       (synagogue, from Hebrew
       <i>
        qahal
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_languages">
        Other languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Other languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Judaeo-Spanish has absorbed some words from the local languages but sometimes hispanicised their form:
       <i>
        bilbilico
       </i>
       (nightingale), from Persian (via Turkish)
       <i>
        bülbül
       </i>
       . It may be compared to the Slavic elements in Yiddish. It is not always clear whether some of these words were introduced before the Expulsion because of the large number of Arabic words in Spanish generally.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">
        Phonology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Phonology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Judaeo-Spanish phonology consists of 27
       <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">
        phonemes
       </a>
       : 22
       <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">
        consonants
       </a>
       and 5
       <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">
        vowels
       </a>
       .
      </p>

        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Close-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close-mid back rounded vowel">
           o
          </a>
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_vowel" title="Open-mid vowel">
          Open-mid
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
         (
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid front unrounded vowel">
           ɛ
          </a>
         </span>
         )
        </td>
        <td>
         (
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open-mid back rounded vowel">
           ɔ
          </a>
         </span>
         )
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/Open_vowel" title="Open vowel">
          Open
         </a>
        </th>
        <td colspan="2">
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open central unrounded vowel">
           a
          </a>
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonological_differences_from_Spanish">
        Phonological differences from Spanish
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Phonological differences from Spanish">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       As exemplified in the Sources section above, much of the phonology of Judaeo-Spanish is similar to that of standard modern Spanish. Here are some exceptions:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        It is claimed that unlike all other non-creole varieties of Spanish, Judaeo-Spanish does not contrast the trill
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /r/
        </span>
        and the tap/flap
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɾ/
        </span>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_21-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-:0-21">
          [19]
         </a>
        </sup>
        . However, that claim is not universally accepted
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
         <a href="#cite_note-22">
          [20]
         </a>
        </sup>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The Spanish
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /nue-/
        </span>
        is
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /mue-/
        </span>
        in some dialects of Judaeo-Spanish:
        <i>
         nuevo, nuestro → muevo, muestro.
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_21-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-:0-21">
          [19]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        The Judaeo-Spanish phoneme inventory includes separate
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [d͡ʒ]
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʒ]
        </span>
        :
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ʒuɾˈnal/
        </span>
        <i>
         ('newspaper')
        </i>
        vs
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /d͡ʒuˈgar/
        </span>
        <i>
         ('to play')
        </i>
        . Neither phoneme is used in modern Spanish.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_21-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-:0-21">
          [19]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        There is a tendency to drop [s] at the end of a word or syllable, as in
        <a href="/wiki/Andalusian_Spanish#Phonological_features" title="Andalusian Spanish">
         Andalusian Spanish
        </a>
        :
        <i>
         Dios
        </i>
        -&gt;
        <i>
         Dio
        </i>
        (God),
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
         <a href="#cite_note-23">
          [21]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <i>
         amargasteis
        </i>
        -&gt;
        <i>
         amargateş
        </i>
        (you have embittered).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Morphology">
        Morphology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Morphology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Judaeo-Spanish is distinguished from other Spanish dialects by the presence of the following features:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Judaeo-Spanish maintains the second-person
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pronouns" title="Pronouns">
         pronouns
        </a>
        <i>
         tú
        </i>
        (informal singular),
        <i>
         vos
        </i>
        (formal singular) and
        <i>
         vosotros
        </i>
        (plural); the third-person
        <i>
         el/eya/eyos/eyas
        </i>
        are also used in the formal register.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_21-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-:0-21">
          [19]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The Spanish pronouns
        <i>
         usted
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         ustedes
        </i>
        do not exist.
       </li>
       <li>
        In verbs, the
        <a href="/wiki/Preterite" title="Preterite">
         preterite
        </a>
        indicates that an action taken once in the past was also completed at some point in the past. That is as opposed to the
        <a href="/wiki/Imperfect" title="Imperfect">
         imperfect
        </a>
        , which refers to any continuous, habitual, unfinished or repetitive past action. Thus, "I ate falafel yesterday" would use the first-person preterite form of eat,
        <i>
         komí
        </i>
        /
        <i>
         comí
        </i>
        but "When I lived in Izmir, I ran five miles every evening" would use the first-person imperfect form,
        <i>
         koría
        </i>
        /
        <i>
         corría
        </i>
        . Though some of the morphology has changed, usage is just as in normative Spanish.
       </li>
       <li>
        In general, Judaeo-Spanish uses the Spanish plural morpheme /-(e)s/. The Hebrew plural endings
        <i>
         /-im/
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         /-ot/
        </i>
        are used with Hebrew loanwords, as well as with a few words from Spanish:
        <i>
         ladrón
        </i>
        (thief):
        <i>
         ladrones/ladroním; ermano
        </i>
        (brother):
        <i>
         ermanos/ermaním.
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
         <a href="#cite_note-24">
          [22]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Similarly, some loaned feminine nouns ending in -á can take either the Spanish or Hebrew plural:
        <i>
         keilá
        </i>
        (synagogue):
        <i>
         keilás/keilot.
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        Judaeo-Spanish contains more gendering cases than standard Spanish, prominently in adjectives, (
        <i>
         grande/-a, inferior/-ra
        </i>
        ) as well as in nouns (
        <i>
         vozas, fuentas
        </i>
        ) and in the interrogative
        <i>
         kualo/kuala.
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_21-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-:0-21">
          [19]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Here is the regular conjugation for the present tense:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Syntax">
        Syntax
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Syntax">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Judaeo-Spanish follows Spanish for most of its syntax. (That is not true of the written calque language involving word-for-word translations from Hebrew, which some scholars refer to as Ladino, as described above.) Like Spanish, it generally follows a subject–verb–object
       <a href="/wiki/Word_order" title="Word order">
        word order
       </a>
       , has a
       <a href="/wiki/Morphosyntactic_alignment" title="Morphosyntactic alignment">
        nominative-accusative alignment
       </a>
       , and is considered a
       <a href="/wiki/Fusional_language" title="Fusional language">
        fusional
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">
        inflected
       </a>
       language.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Orthography">
        Orthography
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Orthography">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rashiscript.PNG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="87" data-file-width="341" height="56" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Rashiscript.PNG/220px-Rashiscript.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Rashiscript.PNG/330px-Rashiscript.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/04/Rashiscript.PNG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rashiscript.PNG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Rashi_script" title="Rashi script">
          Rashi script
         </a>
         , originally used to print the language
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The following systems of writing Judaeo-Spanish have been used or proposed.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Traditionally, especially in Ladino religious texts, Judaeo-Spanish was printed in
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew_writing" title="Hebrew writing">
         Hebrew writing
        </a>
        (especially in
        <a href="/wiki/Rashi_script" title="Rashi script">
         Rashi script
        </a>
        ), a practice that was very common, possibly almost universal, until the 19th century. That was called
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Aljamiado" title="Aljamiado">
          aljamiado
         </a>
        </i>
        , by analogy with the equivalent use of the
        <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">
         Arabic script
        </a>
        . It occasionally persists, especially in religious use. Everyday written records of the language used
        <a href="/wiki/Solitreo" title="Solitreo">
         Solitreo
        </a>
        , a semi-cursive script similar to Rashi script that shifted to square letter for Hebrew/Aramaic words. Solitreo is clearly different from the Ashkenazi Cursive Hebrew used today in Israel, but it is also related to Rashi script. (A comparative table is provided in the article on
        <a href="/wiki/Cursive_Hebrew" title="Cursive Hebrew">
         Cursive Hebrew
        </a>
        .) Hebrew writing of the language freely uses
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matres_lectionis" title="Matres lectionis">
         matres lectionis
        </a>
        : final -a is written with
        <span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', 'Keter Aram Tsova', 'Taamey Ashkenaz', 'Taamey David CLM', 'Taamey Frank CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Keter YG', 'Shofar', 'David CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', 'Simple CLM', 'Nachlieli', 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', Cardo, Alef, 'Noto Serif Hebrew', 'Noto Sans Hebrew', 'David Libre', David, 'Times New Roman', Gisha, Arial, FreeSerif, FreeSans;">
         <a href="/wiki/He_(letter)" title="He (letter)">
          ה
         </a>
        </span>
        ‬ (
        <i>
         heh
        </i>
        ) and
        <span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', 'Keter Aram Tsova', 'Taamey Ashkenaz', 'Taamey David CLM', 'Taamey Frank CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Keter YG', 'Shofar', 'David CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', 'Simple CLM', 'Nachlieli', 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', Cardo, Alef, 'Noto Serif Hebrew', 'Noto Sans Hebrew', 'David Libre', David, 'Times New Roman', Gisha, Arial, FreeSerif, FreeSans;">
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vav_(letter)" title="Vav (letter)">
          ו
         </a>
        </span>
        ‬ (
        <i>
         waw
        </i>
        ) can represent
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /o/
        </span>
        or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /u/
        </span>
        . Both s (
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /s/
        </span>
        ) and x (
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ʃ/
        </span>
        ) are generally written with
        <span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', 'Keter Aram Tsova', 'Taamey Ashkenaz', 'Taamey David CLM', 'Taamey Frank CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Keter YG', 'Shofar', 'David CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', 'Simple CLM', 'Nachlieli', 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', Cardo, Alef, 'Noto Serif Hebrew', 'Noto Sans Hebrew', 'David Libre', David, 'Times New Roman', Gisha, Arial, FreeSerif, FreeSans;">
         <a href="/wiki/Shin_(letter)" title="Shin (letter)">
          ש
         </a>
        </span>
        ‬, as
        <span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', 'Keter Aram Tsova', 'Taamey Ashkenaz', 'Taamey David CLM', 'Taamey Frank CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Keter YG', 'Shofar', 'David CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', 'Simple CLM', 'Nachlieli', 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', Cardo, Alef, 'Noto Serif Hebrew', 'Noto Sans Hebrew', 'David Libre', David, 'Times New Roman', Gisha, Arial, FreeSerif, FreeSans;">
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samech" title="Samech">
          ס
         </a>
        </span>
        ‬ is generally reserved for c before e or i and ç. However, borrowed Hebrew words retain their Hebrew spelling, without vowels.
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">
         Greek alphabet
        </a>
        and the
        <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script" title="Cyrillic script">
         Cyrillic script
        </a>
        were used in the past,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
         <a href="#cite_note-25">
          [23]
         </a>
        </sup>
        but this is rare or nonexistent nowadays.
       </li>
       <li>
        In Turkey, Judaeo-Spanish is most commonly written in the
        <a href="/wiki/Turkish_alphabet" title="Turkish alphabet">
         Turkish variant
        </a>
        of the
        <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">
         Latin alphabet
        </a>
        . That may be the most widespread system in use today, as following the decimation of Sephardic communities throughout much of Europe (particularly in Greece and the
        <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">
         Balkans
        </a>
        ) during the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holocaust" title="Holocaust">
         Holocaust
        </a>
        , the greatest proportion of speakers remaining were Turkish Jews. However, the Judaeo-Spanish page of the Turkish Jewish newspaper
        <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Ealom" title="Şalom">
         Şalom
        </a>
        now uses the Israeli system.
       </li>
       <li>
        The Israeli
        <a href="/wiki/Autoridad_Nasionala_del_Ladino" title="Autoridad Nasionala del Ladino">
         Autoridad Nasionala del Ladino
        </a>
        promotes a
        <a href="/wiki/Phonetic_transcription" title="Phonetic transcription">
         phonetic transcription
        </a>
        in the
        <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">
         Latin alphabet
        </a>
        , without making any concessions to Spanish orthography, and uses the transcription in its publication
        <a href="/wiki/Aki_Yerushalayim" title="Aki Yerushalayim">
         Aki Yerushalayim
        </a>
        . The songs
        <i>
         Non komo muestro Dio
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         Por una ninya
        </i>
        ,
        <a href="#Songs">
         below
        </a>
        , and the text in the
        <a href="#Samples">
         sample
        </a>
        paragraph, below, are written using the system.
       </li>
       <li>
        Works published in Spain usually adopt the standard orthography of modern Spanish to make them easier for modern Spaniards to read.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
         <a href="#cite_note-26">
          [24]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The editions often use diacritics to show where the Judaeo-Spanish pronunciation differs from modern Spanish.
       </li>
       <li>
        Perhaps more conservative and less popular, others, including Pablo Carvajal Valdés suggest for Judaeo-Spanish to adopt the orthography that was used at the time of the Expulsion.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Arguments_for_and_against_the_1492_orthography">
        Arguments for and against the 1492 orthography
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Arguments for and against the 1492 orthography">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Original_research" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="40" data-file-width="40" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" width="40"/>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          This article
          <b>
           possibly contains
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research">
            original research
           </a>
          </b>
          .
          <span class="hide-when-compact">
           Please
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit">
            improve it
           </a>
           by
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
            verifying
           </a>
           the claims made and adding
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#Inline_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">
            inline citations
           </a>
           . Statements consisting only of original research should be removed.
          </span>
          <small>
           <i>
            (July 2012)
           </i>
          </small>
          <small class="hide-when-compact">
           <i>
            (
            <a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">
             Learn how and when to remove this template message
            </a>
            )
           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The Spanish orthography of 1492 was later standardized and eventually changed by a series of
       <a href="/wiki/Spelling_reform#Spanish" title="Spelling reform">
        spelling reforms
       </a>
       from 1726 to 1815 to become the spelling of modern Spanish. Judaeo-Spanish has retained some of the pronunciations that had become archaic in Standard Spanish by then. Adopting 15th-century Spanish orthography, which was similar to modern
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_orthography" title="Portuguese orthography">
        Portuguese orthography
       </a>
       , would therefore closely fit the pronunciation of Judaeo-Spanish.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The old spelling would reflect
        <ul>
         <li>
          the
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /s/
          </span>
          (originally
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /ts/
          </span>
          ) –
          <i>
           c
          </i>
          (before
          <i>
           e
          </i>
          and
          <i>
           i
          </i>
          ) and
          <i>
           ç
          </i>
          (
          <a href="/wiki/Cedilla" title="Cedilla">
           cedilla
          </a>
          ), as in
          <i>
           caça
          </i>
          ,
         </li>
         <li>
          the
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /s/
          </span>
          –
          <i>
           ss
          </i>
          , as in
          <i>
           passo
          </i>
          , and
         </li>
         <li>
          the
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /ʃ/
          </span>
          –
          <i>
           x
          </i>
          , as in
          <i>
           dixo
          </i>
          .
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        The letter
        <i>
         j
        </i>
        would be retained but only when the pronunciation is
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ʒ/
        </span>
        in Judaeo-Spanish, as in
        <i>
         mujer
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The spelling of
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /z/
        </span>
        , originally
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /dz/
        </span>
        , as
        <i>
         z
        </i>
        would be restored in words like
        <i>
         fazer
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         dezir
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The difference between
        <i>
         b
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         v
        </i>
        would be made phonetically, as in Old Spanish, rather than in accordance with the Latin
        <a href="/wiki/Etymology" title="Etymology">
         etymology
        </a>
        , as in modern Spanish. For example, Latin
        <i>
         <small>
          DEBET
         </small>
        </i>
        &gt;, in modern Spanish
        <i>
         debe
        </i>
        , would return to its Old Spanish spelling,
        <i>
         deve
        </i>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Some old spellings could be restored for the sake of historical interest, rather than to reflect Judaeo-Spanish phonology:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The old
        <a href="/wiki/Digraph_(orthography)" title="Digraph (orthography)">
         digraphs
        </a>
        <i>
         ch
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         ph
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         th
        </i>
        (today
        <i>
         c
        </i>
        /
        <i>
         qu
        </i>
        –
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /k/
        </span>
        ,
        <i>
         f
        </i>
        –
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /f/
        </span>
        and
        <i>
         t
        </i>
        –
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /t/
        </span>
        in standard Spanish respectively), formally abolished in 1803, would be used in words like
        <i>
         orthographía
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         theología
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Latin and Old Spanish
        <i>
         q
        </i>
        before words like
        <i>
         quando
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         quanto
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         qual
        </i>
        (modern Spanish
        <i>
         cuando
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         cuanto
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         cual
        </i>
        ) would also be used.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Supporters argue that classical and Golden Age Spanish literature might gain renewed interest and better appreciation and understanding.
      </p>
      <p>
       It remains uncertain how to treat sounds that Old Spanish spelling failed to render phonetically:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The
        <i>
         s
        </i>
        between vowels, as in
        <i>
         casa
        </i>
        , was probably pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /z/
        </span>
        in Old Spanish, as it is in Judaeo-Spanish. The same is true of
        <i>
         s
        </i>
        before
        <i>
         m
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         d
        </i>
        and other voiced consonants, as in
        <i>
         mesmo
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         desde
        </i>
        . Supporters of Carvajal's proposal are unsure about whether it should be written
        <i>
         s
        </i>
        , as in Old Spanish, or
        <i>
         z
        </i>
        in accordance with pronunciation.
       </li>
       <li>
        The distinctive Judaeo-Spanish pronunciation of
        <i>
         s
        </i>
        as
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ʃ/
        </span>
        before a
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /k/
        </span>
        sound, as in
        <i>
         buscar
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         cosquillas
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         mascar
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         pescar
        </i>
        , or in
        <i>
         -is
        </i>
        , as in
        <i>
         séis
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         favláis
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         sois
        </i>
        , is probably derived from Portuguese: it is uncertain whether it occurred in Old Spanish. It is debated whether that should be written
        <i>
         s
        </i>
        , as in Old Spanish, or
        <i>
         x
        </i>
        , in accordance with the sound.
       </li>
       <li>
        There is some dispute about the Spanish
        <i>
         ll
        </i>
        , which in Judaeo-Spanish (as in most areas of Spain) is pronounced like a
        <i>
         y
        </i>
        . Following Old Spanish orthography, it should be written
        <i>
         ll
        </i>
        ; however, Judaeo-Spanish often writes it
        <i>
         y
        </i>
        in Ladino to avoid ambiguity and reflect the Hebrew spelling. The conservative option is to follow the etymology:
        <i>
         caballero
        </i>
        but
        <i>
         Mayorca
        </i>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
         <a href="#cite_note-27">
          [note 2]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        It is uncertain how loanwords from Hebrew and other languages should be rendered.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Aki_Yerushalayim_orthography">
        <i>
         Aki Yerushalayim
        </i>
        orthography
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Aki Yerushalayim orthography">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Aki_Yerushalayim" title="Aki Yerushalayim">
         Aki Yerushalayim
        </a>
       </i>
       magazine, owned by
       <a href="/wiki/Autoridad_Nasionala_del_Ladino" title="Autoridad Nasionala del Ladino">
        Autoridad Nasionala del Ladino
       </a>
       , promotes the following orthography:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable Unicode">
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         Letter
        </th>
        <td>
         <b>
          A a
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          B b
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          Ch ch
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          D d
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          Dj dj
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          E e
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          F f
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>

         </b>
        </td>

         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          I i
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          J j
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>

         </b>

        </td>

         </b>

         </b>
        </td>
        <td>

         </b>

         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>

         </b>
        </td>
        <td>

         </b>
        </td>
        <td>

         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>

         </b>
        </td>
        <td>

         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>

         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          X x
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          Y y
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          Z z
         </b>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">
          IPA
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
         [a]
        </td>
        <td>
         [b~β]
        </td>
        <td>
         [t͡ʃ]
        </td>
        <td>
         [d~ð]
        </td>
        <td>
         [d͡ʒ]
        </td>
        <td>
         [e]
        </td>
        <td>
         [f]
        </td>

         [g~ɣ]
        </td>
        <td>
         [x]
        </td>
        <td>
         [i~j]
        </td>
        <td>
         [ʒ]
        </td>
        <td>
         [k]
        </td>
        <td>
         [l]
        </td>
        <td>
         [m]
        </td>
        <td>
         [n~ŋ]
        </td>
        <td>
         [ɲ]
        </td>
        <td>
         [o]
        </td>
        <td>
         [p]
        </td>
        <td>
         [r~ɾ]
        </td>
        <td>
         [s]
        </td>
        <td>
         [ʃ]
        </td>
        <td>
         [t]
        </td>
        <td>
         [u~w]
        </td>
        <td>
         [v]
        </td>
        <td>
         [gz]
        </td>
        <td>
         [j]
        </td>
        <td>
         [z]
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <ul>
       <li>
        A dot is written between
        <i>
         s
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         h
        </i>
        (s·h) to represent [sx] to avoid confusion with [ʃ]:
        <i>
         es·huenyo
        </i>
        [esˈxweɲo] (dream).
       </li>
       <li>
        Unlike Spanish, stressed diacritics are not represented.
       </li>
       <li>
        Loanwords and foreign names retain their original spelling, and
        <i>
         q
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         w
        </i>
        would be used only for such words.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hebrew_orthography">
        Hebrew orthography
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Hebrew orthography">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Judeo-Spanish is traditionally written in a Hebrew-based script, specially in
       <a href="/wiki/Rashi_script" title="Rashi script">
        Rashi script
       </a>
       . The Hebrew orthography is not regulated, but sounds are generally represented by the following letters:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable Unicode" dir="rtl">
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         Square letter
        </th>
        <td>
         <b>
          א
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ב
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ב׳
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ג
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ג׳
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ד
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ה
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ו
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ז
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ז׳
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ח
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ט
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          י
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          יי
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          כ/-ך
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ל
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          מ/-ם
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          נ/-ן
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ניי
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ס
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ע
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          פ/-ף
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          פ׳/-ף׳
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          צ/-ץ
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ק
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ר
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ש
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          ת
         </b>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr align="center">
        <th>
         Rashi letter
        </th>
