          <img alt="Hebrew letter Kuf Rashi.png" data-file-height="35" data-file-width="40" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Hebrew_letter_Kuf_Rashi.png/15px-Hebrew_letter_Kuf_Rashi.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Hebrew_letter_Kuf_Rashi.png/23px-Hebrew_letter_Kuf_Rashi.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Hebrew_letter_Kuf_Rashi.png/30px-Hebrew_letter_Kuf_Rashi.png 2x" width="15"/>
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hebrew_letter_Resh_Rashi.png">
          <img alt="Hebrew letter Resh Rashi.png" data-file-height="35" data-file-width="40" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Hebrew_letter_Resh_Rashi.png/15px-Hebrew_letter_Resh_Rashi.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Hebrew_letter_Resh_Rashi.png/23px-Hebrew_letter_Resh_Rashi.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Hebrew_letter_Resh_Rashi.png/30px-Hebrew_letter_Resh_Rashi.png 2x" width="15"/>
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hebrew_letter_Shin_Rashi.png">
          <img alt="Hebrew letter Shin Rashi.png" data-file-height="35" data-file-width="40" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Hebrew_letter_Shin_Rashi.png/15px-Hebrew_letter_Shin_Rashi.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Hebrew_letter_Shin_Rashi.png/23px-Hebrew_letter_Shin_Rashi.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Hebrew_letter_Shin_Rashi.png/30px-Hebrew_letter_Shin_Rashi.png 2x" width="15"/>
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hebrew_letter_Taf_Rashi.png">
          <img alt="Hebrew letter Taf Rashi.png" data-file-height="35" data-file-width="40" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Hebrew_letter_Taf_Rashi.png/15px-Hebrew_letter_Taf_Rashi.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Hebrew_letter_Taf_Rashi.png/23px-Hebrew_letter_Taf_Rashi.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Hebrew_letter_Taf_Rashi.png/30px-Hebrew_letter_Taf_Rashi.png 2x" width="15"/>
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       In the medieval
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" title="Iberian peninsula">
        Iberian peninsula
       </a>
       , now Spain and Portugal, Jews spoke a variety of Romance dialects. Following the 1490s expulsion from Spain
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expulsion_of_the_Jews_from_Portugal" title="Expulsion of the Jews from Portugal">
        and Portugal
       </a>
       , most the Iberian Jews resettled in the
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
        Ottoman Empire
       </a>
       , Jews in the Ottoman
       <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">
        Balkans
       </a>
       , Turkey,
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">
        Middle East
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">
        North Africa
       </a>
       (especially
       <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">
        Morocco
       </a>
       ) and developed their own Romance dialects, with some influence from Hebrew and other languages, which became what is now known as Judaeo-Spanish. Later on, some Portuguese Jews also settled in France, Italy, the
       <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Netherlands" title="Habsburg Netherlands">
        Netherlands
       </a>
       and England, establishing small groups in those nations as well, but these spoke early modern Spanish or Portuguese rather than Judaeo-Spanish.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jews_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Jews in the Middle Ages">
        Jews in the Middle Ages
       </a>
       were instrumental in the development of Spanish into a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prestige_language" title="Prestige language">
        prestige language
       </a>
       . Erudite Jews translated Arabic and Hebrew works, often translated earlier from Greek, into Spanish. Christians
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Translations_into_Latin_(c._1050-c.1250)" title="Translations into Latin (c. 1050-c.1250)">
        translated them again into Latin
       </a>
       for transmission to Europe.
      </p>
      <p>
       Until recent times, the language was widely spoken throughout the Balkans, Turkey, the Middle East and North Africa, as Judaeo-Spanish had been brought there by the Jewish refugees.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The contact among Jews of different regions and languages, including
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
        Catalan
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leonese_language" title="Leonese language">
        Leonese
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
        Portuguese
       </a>
       developed a unified dialect, differing in some aspects from the Spanish norm that was forming simultaneously in Spain, but some of the mixing may have occurred in exile rather than in the Iberian Peninsula. The language was known as
       <i>
        Yahudice
       </i>
       (Jewish language) in the Ottoman Empire. In the late 18th century, Ottoman poet Enderunlu Fazıl (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fazyl_bin_Tahir_Enderuni" title="Fazyl bin Tahir Enderuni">
        Fazyl bin Tahir Enderuni
       </a>
       ) wrote in his
       <i>
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Zenanname&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Zenanname (page does not exist)">
         Zenanname
        </a>
       </i>
       : "Castilians speak the Jewish language but they are not Jews."
      </p>
      <p>
       The closeness and mutual comprehensibility between Judaeo-Spanish and Spanish favoured trade among Sephardim, often relatives, from the Ottoman Empire to the Netherlands and the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Converso" title="Converso">
         conversos
        </a>
       </i>
       of the Iberian Peninsula.
      </p>
      <p>
       After the Expulsion, Jews of mostly-Portuguese descent were one of the influences in
       <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Brazil" title="Dutch Brazil">
        Dutch Brazil
       </a>
       on
       <a href="/wiki/Papiamento" title="Papiamento">
        Papiamento
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/West_Iberian_languages" title="West Iberian languages">
        West Iberian
       </a>
       creole that is spoken in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dutch_West_Indies" title="Dutch West Indies">
        Dutch West Indies
       </a>
       and believed to
       <a href="/wiki/Stratum_(linguistics)" title="Stratum (linguistics)">
        be derived
       </a>
       from Portuguese,
       <a href="/wiki/Judaeo-Portuguese" title="Judaeo-Portuguese">
        Judaeo-Portuguese
       </a>
       , Spanish and Judaeo-Spanish.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Over time, a corpus of literature, both liturgical and secular, developed. Early literature was limited to translations from Hebrew. At the end of the 17th century, Hebrew was disappearing as the vehicle for rabbinic instruction. Thus, a literature appeared in the 18th century, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Me%27am_Lo%27ez" title="Me'am Lo'ez">
        Me'am Lo'ez
       </a>
       and poetry collections. By the end of the 19th century, the Sephardim in the Ottoman Empire studied in schools of the
       <a href="/wiki/Alliance_Isra%C3%A9lite_Universelle" title="Alliance Israélite Universelle">
        Alliance Israélite Universelle
       </a>
       . French became the language for foreign relations, as it did for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maronite" title="Maronite">
        Maronites
       </a>
       , and Judaeo-Spanish drew from French for neologisms. New. secular genres appeared, with more than 300 journals, history, theatre, biographies.
      </p>
      <p>
       Given the relative isolation of many communities, a number of regional dialects of Judaeo-Spanish appeared, many with only limited mutual comprehensibility, largely because of the adoption of large numbers of loanwords from the surrounding populations, including, depending on the location of the community, from Greek, Turkish, Arabic and, in the
       <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">
        Balkans
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">
        Slavic languages
       </a>
       , especially
       <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_language" title="Bosnian language">
        Bosnian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">
        Bulgarian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Serbo-Croatian" title="Serbo-Croatian">
        Serbo-Croatian
       </a>
       . The borrowing in many Judaeo-Spanish dialects is so heavy that up to 30% of their vocabulary of non-Spanish origin. Some words also passed from Judaeo-Spanish into neighbouring languages. For example, the word
       <i>
        palavra
       </i>
       "word" (
       <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin_vocabulary" title="Vulgar Latin vocabulary">
        Vulgar Latin
       </a>
       = "parabola"; Greek = "parabole"), passed into Turkish, Greek and Romanian
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DER_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-DER-29">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with the meaning "bunk, hokum, humbug, bullshit" in Turkish and Romanian and "big talk, boastful talk" in Greek (compare the English word "palaver").
      </p>
      <p>
       Judaeo-Spanish was the common language of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salonika" title="Salonika">
        Salonika
       </a>
       during the Ottoman period. The city became part of Greece in 1912 and was subsequently renamed Thessaloniki. Despite the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_Thessaloniki" title="Great Fire of Thessaloniki">
        Great Fire of Thessaloniki
       </a>
       , economic oppression by Greek authorities and mass settlement of Christian refugees, the language remained widely spoken in Salonika until the deportation of 50,000 Salonikan Jews in the Holocaust during the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">
        Second World War
       </a>
       . According to the 1928 census, the language had 62,999 native speakers in Greece. The figure drops down to 53,094 native speakers in 1940, but 21,094 citizens "usually" spoke the language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Judaeo-Spanish was also a language used in
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Donmeh" title="Donmeh">
         Donmeh
        </a>
       </i>
       rites (
       <i>
        Dönme
       </i>
       being a Turkish word for "convert" to refer to adepts of
       <a href="/wiki/Sabbatai_Zevi" title="Sabbatai Zevi">
        Sabbatai Tsevi
       </a>
       converting to Islam in the Ottoman Empire). An example is
       <i>
        Sabbatai Tsevi esperamos a ti
       </i>
       . Today, the religious practices and the ritual use of Judaeo-Spanish seems confined to elderly generations.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Castilian colonisation of Northern Africa favoured the role of polyglot Sephards, who bridged between Spanish -colonizers and Arab and Berber speakers.
      </p>
      <p>
       From the 17th to the 19th centuries, Judaeo-Spanish was the predominant Jewish language in the Holy Land, but its dialect was different in some respects from the one in Greece and Turkey. Some families have lived in Jerusalem for centuries and preserve Judaeo-Spanish for cultural and folklore purposes although they now use Hebrew in everyday life.
      </p>
      <p>
       An often-told Sephardic anecdote from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bosnia-Herzegovina" title="Bosnia-Herzegovina">
        Bosnia-Herzegovina
       </a>
       has it that as a Spanish consulate was opened in
       <a href="/wiki/Sarajevo" title="Sarajevo">
        Sarajevo
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Interwar_period" title="Interwar period">
        interwar period
       </a>
       , two Sephardic women passed by. Upon hearing a Catholic priest who was speaking Spanish, they thought that his language meant that he was Jewish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Papo_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Papo-31">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the 20th century, the number of speakers declined sharply: entire communities were murdered in the Holocaust, and the remaining speakers, many of whom emigrated to Israel, adopted Hebrew. The governments of the new
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nation-state" title="Nation-state">
        nation-states
       </a>
       encouraged instruction in the official languages. At the same time, Judaeo-Spanish aroused the interest of philologists, as it conserved language and literature from before the standardisation of Spanish.
      </p>
      <p>
       Judaeo-Spanish is in a serious danger of extinction because many native speakers today are elderly
       <a href="/wiki/Aliyah" title="Aliyah">
        <i>
         olim
        </i>
       </a>
       (immigrants to Israel), who have not transmitted the language to their children or grandchildren. Nevertheless, it is experiencing a minor revival among Sephardic communities, especially in music. In addition, Sephardic communities in several Latin American countries still use Judaeo-Spanish. There, an additional danger is the asimilation to modern Spanish.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Kol_Yisrael" title="Kol Yisrael">
        Kol Yisrael
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-REKA_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-REKA-32">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Radio_Nacional_de_Espa%C3%B1a" title="Radio Nacional de España">
        Radio Nacional de España
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-REE_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-REE-33">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       hold regular radio broadcasts in Judaeo-Spanish.
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Law_%26_Order:_Criminal_Intent" title="Law &amp; Order: Criminal Intent">
         Law &amp; Order: Criminal Intent
        </a>
       </i>
       showed an episode, titled "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Murderer_Among_Us" title="A Murderer Among Us">
        A Murderer Among Us
       </a>
       ", with references to the language. Films partially or totally in Judaeo-Spanish include Mexican film
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Novia_que_te_vea_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Novia que te vea (film) (page does not exist)">
        <i>
         Novia que te vea
        </i>
       </a>
       (directed by
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Guita_Schyfter&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Guita Schyfter (page does not exist)">
        Guita Schyfter
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/The_House_on_Chelouche_Street" title="The House on Chelouche Street">
        The House on Chelouche Street
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Every_Time_We_Say_Goodbye_(film)" title="Every Time We Say Goodbye (film)">
        <i>
         Every Time We Say Goodbye
        </i>
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Efforts have been made to gather and publish modern Judaeo-Spanish fables and folktales. In 2001, the
       <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Publication_Society" title="Jewish Publication Society">
        Jewish Publication Society
       </a>
       published the first English translation of Judaeo-Spanish folktales, collected by Matilda Koén-Sarano,
       <i>
        Folktales of Joha, Jewish Trickster: The Misadventures of the Guileful Sephardic Prankster.
       </i>
       A survivor of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auschwitz" title="Auschwitz">
        Auschwitz
       </a>
       , Moshe Ha'elyon, issued his translation into Ladino of the ancient Greek epic
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Odyssey" title="The Odyssey">
        The Odyssey
       </a>
       in 2012, in his 87th year, and he is now translating the sister epic, the
       <a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">
        Iliad
       </a>
       , into his mother tongue.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The language was initially spoken by the
       <a href="/wiki/Sephardic_Jews_in_India" title="Sephardic Jews in India">
        Sephardic Jewish community in India
       </a>
       , but was later replaced with
       <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Malayalam" title="Judeo-Malayalam">
        Judeo-Malayalam
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">
        Literature
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Literature">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The earliest Judeo-Spanish books were religious in nature, mostly created to maintain religious knowledge for exiles who could not read Hebrew; the first of the known texts is
       <i>
        Dinim de shehitah i bedikah (The Rules of Ritual Slaughter and Inspection of Animals;
       </i>
       Istanbul, 1510).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Texts continued to be focussed on philosophical and religious themes, including a large body of rabbinic writings, until the first half of the 19th century. The largest output of secular Judeo-Spanish literature occurred during the latter half of the 19th and the early 20th centuries in the Ottoman Empire. The earliest and most abundant form of secular text was the periodical press: between 1845 and 1939, Ottoman Sephardim published around 300 individual periodical titles.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The proliferation of periodicals gave rise to serialised novels: many of them were rewrites of existing foreign novels into Judeo-Spanish. Unlike the previous scholarly literature, they were intended for a broader audience of educated men and less-educated women alike. They covered a wider range of less weighty content, at times censored to be appropriate for family readings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Popular literature expanded to include love stories and adventure stories, both of which had been absent from Judeo-Spanish literary canon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The literary corpus mesnwhile also expanded to include theatrical plays, poems and other minor genres.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_use">
        Religious use
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Religious use">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The Jewish communities of
       <a href="/wiki/Sarajevo" title="Sarajevo">
        Sarajevo
       </a>
       , Bosnia-Herzegovina, and
       <a href="/wiki/Belgrade" title="Belgrade">
        Belgrade
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">
        Serbia
       </a>
       , still chant part of the Sabbath Prayers (
       <i>
        Mizmor David
       </i>
       ) in Judaeo-Spanish. The Sephardic Synagogue Ezra Bessaroth in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seattle,_Washington" title="Seattle, Washington">
        Seattle, Washington
       </a>
       , United States, was formed by Jews from Turkey and the Greek island of
       <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">
        Rhodes
       </a>
       , and it uses the language in some portions of its
       <a href="/wiki/Shabbat" title="Shabbat">
        Shabbat
       </a>
       services. The
       <a href="/wiki/Siddur" title="Siddur">
        Siddur
       </a>
       is called Zehut Yosef and was written by Hazzan Isaac Azose.
      </p>
      <p>
       At Congregation Etz Ahaim of
       <a href="/wiki/Highland_Park,_New_Jersey" title="Highland Park, New Jersey">
        Highland Park, New Jersey
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       a congregation founded by Sephardic Jews from
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Thessaloniki" title="History of the Jews in Thessaloniki">
        Salonika
       </a>
       , a reader chants the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">
        Aramaic
       </a>
       prayer
       <i>
        B'rikh Shemay
       </i>
       in Judaeo-Spanish before he takes out the
       <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">
        Torah
       </a>
       on
       <a href="/wiki/Shabbat" title="Shabbat">
        Shabbat
       </a>
       . That is known as
       <i>
        Bendichu su Nombre
       </i>
       in Judaeo-Spanish. Additionally, at the end of Shabbat services, the entire congregation sings the well-known Hebrew hymn
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ein_Keloheinu" title="Ein Keloheinu">
         Ein Keloheinu
        </a>
        ,
       </i>
       which is
       <i>
        Non Como Muestro Dio
       </i>
       in Judaeo-Spanish.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Non Como Muestro Dio
       </i>
       is also included, alongside
       <i>
        Ein Keloheinu
       </i>
       , in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Mishkan_T%27filah" title="Mishkan T'filah">
         Mishkan T'filah
        </a>
       </i>
       , the 2007
       <a href="/wiki/Reform_Judaism" title="Reform Judaism">
        Reform
       </a>
       prayerbook.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Rabbi
       <a href="/wiki/Aryeh_Kaplan" title="Aryeh Kaplan">
        Aryeh Kaplan
       </a>
       translated some scholarly religious texts, including
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Me%27am_Loez" title="Me'am Loez">
         Me'am Loez
        </a>
       </i>
       into Hebrew, English or both.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Izmir's grand rabbis
       <a href="/wiki/Haim_Palachi" title="Haim Palachi">
        Haim Palachi
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Palacci" title="Abraham Palacci">
        Abraham Palacci
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Rahamim_Nissim_Palacci" title="Rahamim Nissim Palacci">
        Rahamim Nissim Palacci
       </a>
       all wrote in the language and in Hebrew.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_education_and_use">
        Modern education and use
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Modern education and use">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       As with Yiddish
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Judaeo-Spanish is seeing a minor resurgence in educational interest in colleges across the United States and in Israel.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Almost all
       <a href="/wiki/American_Jews" title="American Jews">
        American Jews
       </a>
       are
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ashkenazi" title="Ashkenazi">
        Ashkenazi
       </a>
       , with a tradition based on Yiddish, rather than Judaeo-Spanish, and so institutions that offer Yiddish are more common. As of 2011
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of Pennsylvania">
        University of Pennsylvania
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Tufts_University" title="Tufts University">
        Tufts University
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       offered Judaeo-Spanish courses among colleges in the United States.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In Israel, Moshe David Gaon Center for Ladino Culture at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ben-Gurion_University" title="Ben-Gurion University">
        Ben-Gurion University
       </a>
       of the Negev is leading the way in education (language and literature courses, Community oriented activities) and research (a yearly scientific journal, international congresses and conferences etc.).
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew_University" title="Hebrew University">
        Hebrew University
       </a>
       also offers courses.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Complutense_University_of_Madrid" title="Complutense University of Madrid">
        Complutense University of Madrid
       </a>
       also used to have courses.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Prof. David Bunis taught Ladino at the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Washington" title="University of Washington">
        University of Washington
       </a>
       , in Seattle during the 2013–14 academic year.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In Spain, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_Royal_Academy" title="Spanish Royal Academy">
        Spanish Royal Academy
       </a>
       (RAE) in 2017 announced plans to create a Judeo-Spanish branch in Israel in addition to 23 existing academies, in various Spanish-speaking countries, that are associated in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Association_of_Spanish_Language_Academies" title="Association of Spanish Language Academies">
        Association of Spanish Language Academies
       </a>
       . Its stated purpose is to preserve Judaeo-Spanish. The move was seen as another step to make up for the Expulsion, following the
       <a href="/wiki/Sephardi_Jews#Spanish_citizenship_by_Spanish_Sephardic_descent" title="Sephardi Jews">
        offer of Spanish citizenship to Sephards
       </a>
       who had some connection with Spain.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rae_9-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rae-9">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Songs">
        Songs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Songs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Folklorists have been collecting
       <i>
        romances
       </i>
       and other folk songs, some dating from before the expulsion. Many religious songs in Judeo-Spanish are translations of Hebrew, usually with a different tune. For example, here is
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ein_Keloheinu" title="Ein Keloheinu">
         Ein Keloheinu
        </a>
       </i>
       in Judeo-Spanish:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        Non komo muestro Dio,
       </dd>
       <dd>
        Non komo muestro Sinyor,
       </dd>
       <dd>
        Non komo muestro Rey,
       </dd>
       <dd>
        Non komo muestro Salvador.
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         etc.
        </i>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Other songs relate to secular themes such as love:
      </p>
      <table>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Adio, kerida
        </th>
       </tr>

         <br/>

          <br/>

          <br/>
          Para amar segundo.
          <br/>
          Korason ella no te dió
          <br/>
          Para amar segundo.
         </p>
         <p>
          Adio,
          <br/>

          <br/>
          No kero la vida,
          <br/>

          <br/>

          <br/>
          Adio kerida,
          <br/>
          No kero la vida,
          <br/>
          Me l'amargates tu.
          <br/>
         </p>
        </td>

         Va, bushkate otro amor,
         <br/>

          Aharva otras puertas,
          <br/>

          <br/>
          Ke para mi sos muerta.
          <br/>
          Aspera otro ardor,
          <br/>

         </p>

          Adio,
          <br/>

         <br/>

         <br/>
         my sorrows are so great
         <br/>

        </td>

         <br/>

         <br/>
         ay morenas en el mundo
         <br/>
         ke kemaron Selanik.
        </td>

         Don't hold your sorrows, young girl,
         <br/>
         for you are white like bread,
         <br/>
         there are dark girls in the world
         <br/>
         who set fire to
         <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">
          Thessaloniki
         </a>
         .
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
        </td>
       </tr>

         </i>

         <i>
          (translation)
         </i>
        </th>

         Quando el Rey Nimrod al campo salía
         <br/>
         mirava en el cielo y en la estrellería
         <br/>
         vido una luz santa en la
         <a href="/wiki/Jewish_quarter_(diaspora)" title="Jewish quarter (diaspora)">
          djudería
         </a>
         <br/>

        </td>

         When King
         <a href="/wiki/Nimrod" title="Nimrod">

         </a>
         was going out to the fields
         <br/>

         <br/>
         He saw a holy light in the Jewish quarter
         <br/>
         [A sign] that
         <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">
          Abraham
         </a>
         , our father, must have been born.
        </td>

         <br/>
         Blessed Father, light of Israel.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>

         Luego a las comadres encomendava
         <br/>

         <br/>

         <br/>

         La mujer de Terach quedó prenyada
         <br/>
         y de día en día le preguntava
         <br/>
         ¿De qué teneix la cara tan demudada?
         <br/>
         ella ya sabía el bien que tenía.
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terach" title="Terach">
          Terach
         </a>
         's wife was pregnant
         <br/>
         and each day he would ask her
         <br/>
         Why do you look so distraught?
         <br/>
         She already knew very well what she had.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Avraham Avinu, Padre querido,
         <br/>
         Padre bendicho, luz de Yisrael.
        </td>
        <td>
         Abraham Avinu, dear father
         <br/>
         Blessed Father, light of Israel.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         En fin de nueve meses parir quería
         <br/>
         iva caminando por campos y vinyas,
         <br/>
         a su marido tal ni le descubría
         <br/>
         topó una meara, allí lo pariría
        </td>
        <td>
         After nine months she wanted to give birth
         <br/>
         She was walking through the fields and vineyards
         <br/>
         Such would not even reach her husband
         <br/>
         She found a manger; there, she would give birth.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Avraham Avinu, Padre querido,
         <br/>
         Padre bendicho, luz de Yisrael.
        </td>
        <td>
         Abraham Avinu, dear father
         <br/>
         Blessed Father, light of Israel.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         En aquella hora el nascido avlava
         <br/>
         "Andavos mi madre, de la meara
         <br/>
         yo ya topó quen me alexara
         <br/>
         mandará del cielo quen me accompanyará
         <br/>
         porque so criado del Dio bendicho."
        </td>
        <td>
         In that hour the newborn was speaking
         <br/>
         'Get away of the manger, my mother
         <br/>
         I will somebody to take me out
         <br/>
         He will send from the heaven the one that will go with me
         <br/>
         Because I am raised by the blessed God.'
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Avraham Avinu, Padre querido,
         <br/>
         Padre bendicho, luz de Yisrael
        </td>
        <td>
         Abraham Avinu, dear father
         <br/>
         Blessed Father, light of Israel.
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Anachronistically, Abraham—who in the Bible is the very first Hebrew and the ancestor of all who followed, hence his appellation "Avinu" (Our Father)—is in the Judeo-Spanish song born already in the "djudería" (modern Spanish:
       <i>
        judería
       </i>
       ), the Jewish quarter. This makes Terach and his wife into Hebrews, as are the parents of other babies killed by Nimrod. In essence, unlike its Biblical model, the song is about a Hebrew community persecuted by a cruel king and witnessing the birth of a miraculous saviour—a subject of obvious interest and attraction to the Jewish people who composed and sang it in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_Spain" title="Medieval Spain">
        Medieval Spain
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The song attributes to Abraham elements that are from the story of
       <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">
        Moses
       </a>
       's birth, the cruel king killing innocent babies, with the midwives ordered to kill them, the 'holy light' in the Jewish area, as well as from the careers of
       <a href="/wiki/Shadrach,_Meshach,_and_Abednego" title="Shadrach, Meshach, and Abednego">
        Shadrach, Meshach, and Abednego
       </a>
       who emerged unscathed from the fiery furnace.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nimrod_(king)" title="Nimrod (king)">
        Nimrod
       </a>
       is thus made to conflate the role and attributes of two archetypal cruel and persecuting kings:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nebuchadnezzar" title="Nebuchadnezzar">
        Nebuchadnezzar
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">
        Pharaoh
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Selected_words_by_origin">
        Selected words by origin
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Selected words by origin">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">

      <p>
       <i>
        Words derived from Arabic
       </i>
       :
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <b>
         Alforría
        </b>
        – "liberty", "freedom"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Alhát
        </b>
        – "Sunday"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Atemar
        </b>
        – to terminate
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Saraf
        </b>
        – "money changer"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Shara
        </b>
        – "wood"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Ziara
        </b>
        – "cemetery visit"
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       <i>
        Words derived from Hebrew
       </i>
       :
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <b>
         Alefbet
        </b>
        – "alphabet" (from the Hebrew names of the first two letters of the alphabet)
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Anav
        </b>
        – "humble", "obedient"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Arón
        </b>
        – "grave"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Atakanear
        </b>
        – to arrange
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Badkar
        </b>
        – to reconsider
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Beraxa
        </b>
        – "blessing"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Din
        </b>
        – "religious law"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Kal
        </b>
        – "community", "synagogue"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Kamma
        </b>
        – to ask "how much?", "how many?"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Maaráv
        </b>
        – "west"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Maasé
        </b>
        – "story", "event"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Maabe
        </b>
        – "deluge", "downpour", "torrent"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Mazal
        </b>
        – "star", "destiny"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Met
        </b>
        – "dead"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Niftar
        </b>
        – "dead"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Purimlik
        </b>
        – "Purim present" (Derived from the Hebrew "Purim" + Turkic ending "-lik")
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Sedaka
        </b>
        – "charity"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Tefilá
        </b>
        – "prayer"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Zahut
        </b>
        – "blessing"
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       <i>
        Words derived from Persian
       </i>
       :
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <b>
         Chay
        </b>
        – "tea"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Chini
        </b>
        – "plate"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Paras
        </b>
        – "money"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Shasheo
        </b>
        – "dizziness"
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       <i>
        Words derived from Portuguese
       </i>
       :
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <b>
         Abastádo
        </b>
        – "almighty", "omnipotent" (referring to God)
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Aínda
        </b>
        – "yet"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Chapeo
        </b>
        – "hat"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Preto
        </b>
        – "black" (in color)
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Trocar
        </b>
        – to change
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       <i>
        Words derived from Turkish
       </i>
       :
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <b>
         Balta
        </b>
        – "axe"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Biterear
        </b>
        – to terminate
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Boyadear
        </b>
        – to paint, color
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Innat
        </b>
        – "whim"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Kolay
        </b>
        – "easy"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Kushak
        </b>
        – "belt", "girdle"
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Maalé
        </b>
        – "street", "quarters", "neighbourhood";
        <b>
         Maalé yahudí
        </b>
        – Jewish quarters
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_singers">
        Modern singers
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Judaeo-Spanish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Modern singers">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Charles" title="Jennifer Charles">
        Jennifer Charles
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Oren_Bloedow" title="Oren Bloedow">
        Oren Bloedow
       </a>
       from the New York-based band
       <a href="/wiki/Elysian_Fields_(band)" title="Elysian Fields (band)">
        Elysian Fields
       </a>
       released a CD in 2001 called La Mar Enfortuna, which featured modern versions of traditional Sephardic songs, many sung by Charles in Judeo-Spanish. The American singer Tanja Solnik has released several award-winning albums that feature songs in the languages:
       <i>
        From Generation to Generation: A Legacy of Lullabies
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Lullabies and Love Songs
       </i>
       . There are a number of groups in Turkey that sing in Judeo-Spanish, notably
       <i>
        Janet – Jak Esim Ensemble
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Sefarad
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Los Pasharos Sefaradis
       </i>
       and the children's chorus
       <i>
        Las Estreyikas d'Estambol
       </i>
       . There is a Brazilian-born singer of Sephardic origins,
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2012)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Fortuna_(Brazilian_singer)" title="Fortuna (Brazilian singer)">
        Fortuna
       </a>
       , who researches and plays Judeo-Spanish music.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Jewish Bosnian-American musician
       <a href="/wiki/Flory_Jagoda" title="Flory Jagoda">
        Flory Jagoda
       </a>
       recorded two CDs of music taught to her by her grandmother, a Sephardic folk singer, among a larger discography.
      </p>
      <p>
       The cantor
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.ramontasat.com" rel="nofollow">
        Dr. Ramón Tasat
       </a>
       , who learned Judeo-Spanish at his grandmother's knee in
       <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">
        Buenos Aires
       </a>
       , has recorded many songs in the language, with three of his CDs focusing primarily on that music.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Israeli singer
       <a href="/wiki/Yasmin_Levy" title="Yasmin Levy">
        Yasmin Levy
       </a>
       has also brought a new interpretation to the traditional songs by incorporating more "modern" sounds of Andalusian
       <a href="/wiki/Flamenco" title="Flamenco">
        Flamenco
       </a>
       . Her work revitalising Sephardi music has earned Levy the
       <a href="/wiki/Anna_Lindh_Memorial_Fund" title="Anna Lindh Memorial Fund">
        Anna Lindh Euro-Mediterranean Foundation Award
       </a>
       for promoting cross-cultural dialogue between musicians from three cultures:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In Yasmin Levy's own words:
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        <i>
         I am proud to combine the two cultures of Ladino and flamenco, while mixing in Middle Eastern influences. I am embarking on a 500 years old musical journey, taking Ladino to Andalusia and mixing it with flamenco, the style that still bears the musical memories of the old Moorish and Jewish-Spanish world with the sound of the Arab world. In a way it is a ‘musical reconciliation’ of history.
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
         <a href="#cite_note-54">
          [51]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       Notable music groups performing in Judeo-Spanish include
       <a href="/wiki/Voice_of_the_Turtle" title="Voice of the Turtle">
        Voice of the Turtle
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Oren_Bloedow" title="Oren Bloedow">
        Oren Bloedow
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Charles" title="Jennifer Charles">
        Jennifer Charles
       </a>
       ' "La Mar Enfortuna" and
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Vanya_Green&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Vanya Green (page does not exist)">
        Vanya Green
       </a>
       , who was awarded a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fulbright_Fellowship" title="Fulbright Fellowship">
        Fulbright Fellowship
       </a>
       for her research and performance of this music. She was recently selected as one of the top ten world music artists by the We are Listening International World of Music Awards for her interpretations of the music.
      </p>
      <p>
       Robin Greenstein, a New York-based musician, received a federal CETA grant in the 1980s to collect and perform Sephardic Ladino Music under the guidance of the American Jewish Congress. Her mentor was Joe Elias, noted Sephardic singer from Brooklyn. She recorded residents of the Sephardic Home for the Aged, a nursing home in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coney_Island,_New_York" title="Coney Island, New York">
        Coney Island, New York
       </a>
       , singing songs from their childhood. The voices recorded included Victoria Hazan, a well known Sephardic singer who recorded many 78's in Judaeo-Spanish and Turkish from the 1930s and 1940s. Two Judaeo-Spanish songs can be found on her "Songs of the Season" holiday CD, released in 2010 on Windy Records.
      </p>
      <p>
       German band
       <a href="/wiki/In_Extremo" title="In Extremo">
        In Extremo
       </a>
       also recorded a version of the above-mentioned song Avram Avinu.
      </p>

          <span class="reference-text">
           Speakers use different orthographical conventions depending on their social, educational, national and personal backgrounds, and there is no uniformity in spelling although some established conventions exist. The
           <a href="/wiki/Exonym_and_endonym" title="Exonym and endonym">
            endonym
           </a>

           </i>
           ,

           </i>
           ,
           <i>
            Djudeo-Espagnol
           </i>

           </i>
           ,
           <i>
            Dżudeo-Espańol
           </i>
           ,
           <i>
            Giudeo-Espagnol
           </i>
           ,
           <i>
            Giudeo-Espaneol
           </i>

           <i>
            Xhudeo-Espanjol
           </i>
           ,
           <i>
            Dzsudeo-Eszpanyol
           </i>

            Ġudeo-Espanjol
           </i>
           ,
           <i>
            Ǧudéo-Españól
           </i>

           <i>

           </i>

           </i>

          </i>

        <li id="cite_note-25">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-25">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <i>
           Verba Hispanica
          </i>
          X:
          <a class="external text" href="http://hispanismo.cervantes.es/documentos/smidX.pdf" rel="nofollow">
           Los problemas del estudio de la lengua sefardí
          </a>
          <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080407074136/http://hispanismo.cervantes.es/documentos/smidX.pdf" rel="nofollow">
           Archived
          </a>
          7 April 2008 at the
          <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">
           Wayback Machine
          </a>
          ., Katja Šmid, Ljubljana, pages 113–124:
          <i>
           Es interesante el hecho que en Bulgaria se imprimieron unas pocas publicaciones en alfabeto cirílico búlgaro y en Grecia en alfabeto griego. [...] Nezirović (1992: 128) anota que también en Bosnia se ha encontrado un documento en que la lengua sefardí está escrita en alfabeto cirilico
          </i>
          . The Nezirović reference is: Nezirović, M.,
          <i>
           Jevrejsko-Španjolska književnost

              </b>
              indicates

              </a>
             </li>
